colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Website</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;">\n<span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.conandoyleestate.com">www<wbr />.conandoyleestate<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Sir Arthur Ignatius Conan Doyle</b> <span class="noexcerpt" style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Knight_of_Justice_of_the_Order_of_St_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight of Justice of the Order of St John">KStJ</a>&#32;<a href="/wiki/Deputy_Lieutenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Deputy Lieutenant">DL</a></span> (22 May 1859&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 7 July 1930) was a British writer best known for his <a href="/wiki/Detective_fiction" title="Detective fiction">detective fiction</a> featuring the character <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</a>. Originally a <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>, in 1887 he published <i><a href="/wiki/A_Study_in_Scarlet" title="A Study in Scarlet">A Study in Scarlet</a></i>, the first of four novels about Holmes and <a href="/wiki/Dr._Watson" title="Dr. Watson">Dr. Watson</a>. In addition, Doyle wrote over fifty short stories featuring the famous detective. The Sherlock Holmes stories are generally considered milestones in the field of <a href="/wiki/Crime_fiction" title="Crime fiction">crime fiction</a>.\n</p><p>Doyle was a prolific writer; his non-Sherlockian works include <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> stories about <a href="/wiki/Professor_Challenger" title="Professor Challenger">Professor Challenger</a> and humorous stories about the Napoleonic soldier <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_Gerard" title="Brigadier Gerard">Brigadier Gerard</a>, as well as plays, romances, poetry, non-fiction and historical novels. One of Doyle\'s early short stories, "<a href="/wiki/J._Habakuk_Jephson%27s_Statement" title="J. Habakuk Jephson&#39;s Statement">J. Habakuk Jephson\'s Statement</a>", helped to popularise the mystery of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Celeste" title="Mary Celeste">Mary Celeste</a></i>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Doyle is often referred to as <b>Sir Arthur Conan Doyle</b> or simply <b>Conan Doyle</b> (implying that "Conan" is part of a compound surname as opposed to his <a href="/wiki/Given_name" title="Given name">given</a> middle name). His baptism entry in the register of <a href="/wiki/St_Mary%27s_Cathedral,_Edinburgh_(Roman_Catholic)" title="St Mary&#39;s Cathedral, Edinburgh (Roman Catholic)">St Mary\'s Cathedral</a>, Edinburgh, gives "Arthur Ignatius Conan" as his given names and "Doyle" as his surname. It also names Michael Conan as his <a href="/wiki/Godparent" title="Godparent">godfather</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The cataloguers of the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> treat "Doyle" alone as his surname.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Steven Doyle, editor of the <i>Baker Street Journal</i>, wrote, "Conan was Arthur\'s middle name. Shortly after he graduated from high school he began using Conan as a sort of surname. But technically his last name is simply \'Doyle\'."<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> When knighted, he was gazetted as Doyle, not under the compound Conan Doyle.<sup id="cite_ref-LGKnight_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LGKnight-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, the actual use of a compound surname is demonstrated by the fact that Doyle\'s second wife was known as Jean Conan Doyle rather than Jean Doyle.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg/170px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg/255px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg/340px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2005" data-file-height="2812" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_Herbert_Rose_Barraud_1893.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Doyle by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Rose_Barraud" title="Herbert Rose Barraud">Herbert Rose Barraud</a>, 1893</div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle was born on 22 May 1859 at 11 Picardy Place, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Brit_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> His father, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Altamont_Doyle" title="Charles Altamont Doyle">Charles Altamont Doyle</a>, was born in England, of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Catholic">Irish Catholic</a> descent, and his mother, Mary (n\xc3\xa9e Foley), was Irish Catholic. His parents married in 1855.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In 1864 the family dispersed because of Charles\'s growing <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, and the children were temporarily housed across Edinburgh. In 1867, the family came together again and lived in squalid <a href="/wiki/Tenement_flat" class="mw-redirect" title="Tenement flat">tenement flats</a> at 3 <a href="/wiki/Sciennes_Place" class="mw-redirect" title="Sciennes Place">Sciennes Place</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Doyle\'s father died in 1893, in the <a href="/wiki/Crichton_Campus" class="mw-redirect" title="Crichton Campus">Crichton Royal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>, after many years of psychiatric illness.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Supported by wealthy uncles, Doyle was sent to England, at the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Preparatory_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Preparatory school (United Kingdom)">preparatory school</a> <a href="/wiki/Stonyhurst_Saint_Mary%27s_Hall" title="Stonyhurst Saint Mary&#39;s Hall">Hodder Place</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stonyhurst" title="Stonyhurst">Stonyhurst</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a> at the age of nine (1868\xe2\x80\x9370). He then went on to <a href="/wiki/Stonyhurst_College" title="Stonyhurst College">Stonyhurst College</a> until 1875. While Doyle was not unhappy at Stonyhurst, he did not have any fond memories since the school was run on medieval principles, with subjects covering rudiments, <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a>, <a href="/wiki/Euclidean_geometry" title="Euclidean geometry">Euclidean geometry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>, and the classics.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Doyle commented later in his life that the academic system could only be excused "on the plea that any exercise, however stupid in itself, forms a sort of mental dumbbell by which one can improve one\'s mind."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> He also found it harsh, citing that instead of compassion and warmth, it favored the threat of <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">corporal punishment</a> and ritual humiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1875 to 1876, he was educated at the Jesuit school <a href="/wiki/Stella_Matutina_(Jesuit_school)" title="Stella Matutina (Jesuit school)">Stella Matutina</a> in <a href="/wiki/Feldkirch,_Vorarlberg" title="Feldkirch, Vorarlberg">Feldkirch</a>, Austria.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His family decided that he would spend a year there with the objective of perfecting his <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> and broadening his academic horizons.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> He later rejected the Catholic faith and became an <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">agnostic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> A source attributed his drift from religion to science and reason to the time spent in the less strict Austrian school.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He also later became a <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">spiritualist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_career">Medical career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medical career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>From 1876 to 1881, Doyle studied medicine at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh_Medical_School" title="University of Edinburgh Medical School">University of Edinburgh Medical School</a>, including periods working in <a href="/wiki/Aston_Manor" title="Aston Manor">Aston</a> (then a town in <a href="/wiki/Warwickshire" title="Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a>, now part of <a href="/wiki/Birmingham" title="Birmingham">Birmingham</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sheffield" title="Sheffield">Sheffield</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruyton-XI-Towns" title="Ruyton-XI-Towns">Ruyton-XI-Towns</a>, Shropshire.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> During that time, he studied practical botany at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Botanic_Garden_Edinburgh" title="Royal Botanic Garden Edinburgh">Royal Botanic Garden</a> in Edinburgh.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> While studying, Doyle began writing short stories. His earliest extant fiction, "The Haunted Grange of Goresthorpe", was unsuccessfully submitted to <i><a href="/wiki/Blackwood%27s_Magazine" title="Blackwood&#39;s Magazine">Blackwood\'s Magazine</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> His first published piece, "<a href="/wiki/The_Mystery_of_Sasassa_Valley" title="The Mystery of Sasassa Valley">The Mystery of Sasassa Valley</a>", a story set in South Africa, was printed in <i><a href="/wiki/Chambers%27s_Edinburgh_Journal" title="Chambers&#39;s Edinburgh Journal">Chambers\'s Edinburgh Journal</a></i> on 6 September 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> On 20 September 1879, he published his first academic article, "<a href="/wiki/Gelsemium" title="Gelsemium">Gelsemium</a> as a Poison" in the <i><a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> a study which <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> regarded as potentially useful in a 21st-century murder investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-bob_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bob-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Professor_Challenger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Professor_Challenger.jpg/170px-Professor_Challenger.jpg" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Professor_Challenger.jpg/255px-Professor_Challenger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Professor_Challenger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="279" data-file-height="421" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Professor_Challenger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Professor_Challenger" title="Professor Challenger">Professor Challenger</a> by Harry Rountree in the novella <i><a href="/wiki/The_Poison_Belt" title="The Poison Belt">The Poison Belt</a></i> published in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Strand_Magazine" title="The Strand Magazine">The Strand Magazine</a></i></div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle was the doctor on the <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> <a href="/wiki/Whaler" title="Whaler">whaler</a> <i>Hope of Peterhead</i> in 1880.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Medicine,_Bachelor_of_Surgery" title="Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery">M.B.,&#160;C.M.</a> after his graduation from university in 1881, he was ship\'s surgeon on the SS <i>Mayumba</i> during a voyage to the <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West African</a> coast.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> He completed his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">Doctor of Medicine</a> degree (an advanced degree in Scotland beyond the usual medical degrees) on the subject of <i><a href="/wiki/Tabes_dorsalis" title="Tabes dorsalis">tabes dorsalis</a></i> in 1885.<sup id="cite_ref-Archive_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Archive-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1882, Doyle joined former classmate George Turnavine Budd as his partner at a medical practice in <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>, but their relationship proved difficult, and Doyle soon left to set up an independent practice.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Arriving in <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a> in June 1882, with less than \xc2\xa310 (\xc2\xa3900 today<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>) to his name, he set up a medical practice at 1 Bush Villas in Elm Grove, <a href="/wiki/Southsea" title="Southsea">Southsea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The practice was not successful. While waiting for patients, Doyle returned to writing fiction.\n</p><p>Doyle was a staunch supporter of compulsory <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> and wrote several articles advocating for the practice and denouncing the views of <a href="/wiki/Vaccine_controversies" title="Vaccine controversies">anti-vaccinators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early 1891, Doyle attempted the study of <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmology" title="Ophthalmology">ophthalmology</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>. He had previously studied at the Portsmouth Eye Hospital to qualify to perform eye tests and prescribe glasses.  Vienna was suggested by his friend Vernon Morris as a place to spend six months and train to be an eye surgeon. Doyle found it too difficult to understand the German medical terms at the classes in Vienna and quickly quit his studies there. For the rest of his two-month stay in Vienna, he pursued other activities, such as <a href="/wiki/Ice_skating" title="Ice skating">ice skating</a> with his wife Louisa and drinking with Brinsley Richards of the London <i>Times</i>. He also wrote <i>The Doings of Raffles Haw</i>.\n</p><p>After visiting <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, he spent a few days in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> observing Edmund Landolt, an expert on diseases of the eye. Within three months of his departure for Vienna, Doyle returned to London. He opened a small office and consulting room at 2 Upper Wimpole Street, or 2 Devonshire Place as it was then. A <a href="/wiki/Westminster_City_Council" title="Westminster City Council">Westminster City Council</a> <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_plaque" title="Commemorative plaque">commemorative plaque</a> is over the front door. He had no patients according to his autobiography and his efforts as an ophthalmologist were a failure.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_career">Literary career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Literary career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_bibliography" title="Arthur Conan Doyle bibliography">Arthur Conan Doyle bibliography</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sherlock_Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Sherlock Holmes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Doyle struggled to find a publisher for his work. His first work featuring Sherlock Holmes and Dr. Watson, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Study_in_Scarlet" title="A Study in Scarlet">A Study in Scarlet</a></i>, was taken by <a href="/wiki/Ward_Lock_%26_Co" title="Ward Lock &amp; Co">Ward Lock &amp; Co</a> on 20 November 1886, giving Doyle \xc2\xa325 (\xc2\xa32500 today) for all rights to the story. The piece appeared one year later in the <i><a href="/wiki/Beeton%27s_Christmas_Annual" title="Beeton&#39;s Christmas Annual">Beeton\'s Christmas Annual</a></i> and received good reviews in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Scotsman" title="The Scotsman">The Scotsman</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Herald" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasgow Herald">Glasgow Herald</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Holmes was partially modelled on his former university teacher <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bell" title="Joseph Bell">Joseph Bell</a>. In 1892, in a letter to Bell, Doyle wrote, "It is most certainly to you that I owe Sherlock Holmes&#160;... round the centre of deduction and inference and observation which I have heard you inculcate I have tried to build up a man,"<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and, in his 1924 autobiography, he remarked, "It is no wonder that after the study of such a character [viz., Bell] I used and amplified his methods when in later life I tried to build up a scientific detective who solved cases on his own merits and not through the folly of the criminal."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a> was able, even in faraway <a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>, to recognise the strong similarity between <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bell" title="Joseph Bell">Joseph Bell</a> and Sherlock Holmes: "My compliments on your very ingenious and very interesting adventures of Sherlock Holmes.&#160;... can this be my old friend Joe Bell?"<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Other authors sometimes suggest additional influences\xe2\x80\x94for instance, the famous <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe">Edgar Allan Poe</a> character <a href="/wiki/C._Auguste_Dupin" title="C. Auguste Dupin">C. Auguste Dupin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Dr. (John) Watson owes his surname, but not any other obvious characteristic, to a Portsmouth medical colleague of Doyle\'s, Dr James Watson.<sup id="cite_ref-Life_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A sequel to <i>A Study in Scarlet</i> was commissioned, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sign_of_the_Four" title="The Sign of the Four">The Sign of the Four</a></i> appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/Lippincott%27s_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Lippincott&#39;s Magazine">Lippincott\'s Magazine</a></i> in February 1890, under agreement with the Ward Lock company. Doyle felt grievously exploited by Ward Lock as an author new to the publishing world and he left them.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Short stories featuring Sherlock Holmes were published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Strand_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Strand Magazine">Strand Magazine</a></i>. Doyle wrote the first five Holmes short stories from his office at 2 Upper Wimpole Street (then known as Devonshire Place), which is now marked by a memorial plaque.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg/170px-Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg/255px-Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cd/Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg 2x" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sherlock_Holmes_Portrait_Paget.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Sherlock Holmes by <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Paget" title="Sidney Paget">Sidney Paget</a>, 1904</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sherlock_Holmes_Statue,_Edinburgh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Sherlock_Holmes_Statue%2C_Edinburgh.JPG/170px-Sherlock_Holmes_Statue%2C_Edinburgh.JPG" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Sherlock_Holmes_Statue%2C_Edinburgh.JPG/255px-Sherlock_Holmes_Statue%2C_Edinburgh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Sherlock_Holmes_Statue%2C_Edinburgh.JPG/340px-Sherlock_Holmes_Statue%2C_Edinburgh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1888" data-file-height="2553" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sherlock_Holmes_Statue,_Edinburgh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sherlock Holmes statue in Edinburgh, erected opposite the birthplace of Doyle, which was demolished c. 1970</div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle\'s attitude towards his most famous creation was ambivalent.<sup id="cite_ref-Life_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In November 1891 he wrote to his mother: "I think of slaying Holmes,&#160;... and winding him up for good and all. He takes my mind from better things." His mother responded, "You won\'t! You can\'t! You mustn\'t!"<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In an attempt to deflect publishers\' demands for more Holmes stories, he raised his price to a level intended to discourage them, but found they were willing to pay even the large sums he asked.<sup id="cite_ref-Life_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> As a result, he became one of the best-paid authors of his time.\n</p><p>In December 1893, to dedicate more of his time to his historical novels, Doyle had Holmes and <a href="/wiki/Professor_Moriarty" title="Professor Moriarty">Professor Moriarty</a> plunge to their deaths together down the <a href="/wiki/Reichenbach_Falls" title="Reichenbach Falls">Reichenbach Falls</a> in the story "<a href="/wiki/The_Final_Problem" title="The Final Problem">The Final Problem</a>". Public outcry, however, led him to feature Holmes in 1901 in the novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hound_of_the_Baskervilles" title="The Hound of the Baskervilles">The Hound of the Baskervilles</a></i>.\n</p><p>In 1903, Doyle published his first Holmes short story in ten years, "<a href="/wiki/The_Adventure_of_the_Empty_House" title="The Adventure of the Empty House">The Adventure of the Empty House</a>", in which it was explained that only Moriarty had fallen, but since Holmes had other dangerous enemies\xe2\x80\x94especially <a href="/wiki/Colonel_Sebastian_Moran" class="mw-redirect" title="Colonel Sebastian Moran">Colonel Sebastian Moran</a>\xe2\x80\x94he had arranged to also be perceived as dead. Holmes was ultimately featured in a total of 56 <a href="/wiki/Short_story" title="Short story">short stories</a>\xe2\x80\x94the last published in 1927\xe2\x80\x94and four novels by Doyle, and has since appeared in <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes_pastiches" title="Sherlock Holmes pastiches">many novels and stories by other authors</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jane_Stanford" title="Jane Stanford">Jane Stanford</a> compares some of Moriarty\'s characteristics to those of the <a href="/wiki/Fenian" title="Fenian">Fenian</a> <a href="/wiki/John_O%27Connor_Power" title="John O&#39;Connor Power">John O\'Connor Power</a>. "The Final Problem" was published the year the Second <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule Movement">Home Rule Bill</a> passed through the House of Commons. "The Valley of Fear" was serialised in 1914, the year Home Rule, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1914" title="Government of Ireland Act 1914">Government of Ireland Act</a> (18 September) was placed on the Statute Book.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_works">Other works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG/220px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG/330px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG/440px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Conan_Doyle_house.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Doyle\'s house in <a href="/wiki/South_Norwood" title="South Norwood">South Norwood</a></div></div></div><p>Doyle\'s first novels were <i><a href="/wiki/The_Mystery_of_Cloomber" title="The Mystery of Cloomber">The Mystery of Cloomber</a></i>, not published until 1888, and the unfinished <i><a href="/wiki/The_Narrative_of_John_Smith" title="The Narrative of John Smith">Narrative of John Smith</a></i>, published only in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-johnsmith_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnsmith-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> He amassed a portfolio of short stories including "The Captain of the Pole-Star" and "J. Habakuk Jephson\'s Statement", both inspired by Doyle\'s time at sea. The latter popularised the mystery of the <i><a href="/wiki/Mary_Celeste" title="Mary Celeste">Mary Celeste</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-hastings_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and added fictional details such as the perfect condition of the ship (which had actually taken on water by the time it was discovered) and its boats remaining on board (the one boat was in fact missing) that have come to dominate popular accounts of the incident.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hastings_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hastings-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Doyle\'s spelling of the ship\'s name as <i>Marie Celeste</i> has become more common in everyday use than the original form.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Between 1888 and 1906, Doyle wrote seven historical novels, which he and many critics regarded as his best work.<sup id="cite_ref-Life_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Life-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> He also authored nine other novels, and later in his career (1912\xe2\x80\x9329) five narratives, two of novel length, featuring the irascible scientist <a href="/wiki/Professor_Challenger" title="Professor Challenger">Professor Challenger</a>. The Challenger stories include what is probably his best-known work after the Holmes oeuvre, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(Conan_Doyle_novel)" title="The Lost World (Conan Doyle novel)">The Lost World</a></i>. His historical novels include <i><a href="/wiki/Sir_Nigel" title="Sir Nigel">Sir Nigel</a></i> and its follow-up <i><a href="/wiki/The_White_Company" title="The White Company">The White Company</a></i>, set in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. He was a prolific author of short stories, including two collections set in <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_era" title="Napoleonic era">Napoleonic times</a> featuring the French character <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_Gerard" title="Brigadier Gerard">Brigadier Gerard</a>.\n</p><p>Doyle\'s stage works include <i>Waterloo</i>, the reminiscences of an English veteran of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, the character of Gregory Brewster being written for <a href="/wiki/Henry_Irving" title="Henry Irving">Henry Irving</a>; <i>The House of Temperley</i>, the plot of which reflects his abiding interest in boxing; <i>The Speckled Band</i>, after the short story "<a href="/wiki/The_Adventure_of_the_Speckled_Band" title="The Adventure of the Speckled Band">The Adventure of the Speckled Band</a>"; and the 1893 collaboration with <a href="/wiki/J.M._Barrie" class="mw-redirect" title="J.M. Barrie">J.M. Barrie</a> on the <i><a href="/wiki/Libretto" title="Libretto">libretto</a></i> of <i><a href="/wiki/Jane_Annie" title="Jane Annie">Jane Annie</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sporting_career">Sporting career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sporting career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>While living in <a href="/wiki/Southsea" title="Southsea">Southsea</a>, the seaside resort of <a href="/wiki/Portsmouth" title="Portsmouth">Portsmouth</a>, Doyle played <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> as a goalkeeper for <a href="/wiki/A._E._Cogswell#Portsmouth_football_connections" title="A. E. Cogswell">Portsmouth Association Football Club</a>, an amateur side, under the pseudonym A. C. Smith.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle was a keen <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">cricketer</a>, and between 1899 and 1907 he played 10 <a href="/wiki/First-class_cricket" title="First-class cricket">first-class</a> matches for the <a href="/wiki/Marylebone_Cricket_Club" title="Marylebone Cricket Club">Marylebone Cricket Club</a> (MCC).<sup id="cite_ref-cric_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cric-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> He also played for the amateur cricket team the <a href="/wiki/Allahakbarries" title="Allahakbarries">Allahakbarries</a> alongside authors <a href="/wiki/J._M._Barrie" title="J. M. Barrie">J. M. Barrie</a> and <a href="/wiki/A._A._Milne" title="A. A. Milne">A. A. Milne</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> His highest score, in 1902 against <a href="/wiki/London_County_Cricket_Club" title="London County Cricket Club">London County</a>, was 43. He was an occasional bowler who took just one first-class wicket, although one of the highest pedigree as it was <a href="/wiki/W._G._Grace" title="W. G. Grace">W. G. Grace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle was an amateur <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SI_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SI-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 1909, he was invited to referee the <a href="/wiki/James_J._Jeffries" title="James J. Jeffries">James Jeffries</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Jack_Johnson_(boxer)" title="Jack Johnson (boxer)">Jack Johnson</a> heavyweight championship fight in <a href="/wiki/Reno,_Nevada" title="Reno, Nevada">Reno, Nevada</a>. Doyle wrote, "I was much inclined to accept...though my friends pictured me as winding up with a revolver at one ear and a razor at the other. However, the distance and my engagements presented a final bar."<sup id="cite_ref-SI_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SI-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also a keen <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golfer</a>, Doyle was elected captain of the Crowborough Beacon Golf Club in <a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a> for 1910. He had moved to Little Windlesham house in <a href="/wiki/Crowborough" title="Crowborough">Crowborough</a> with Jean Leckie, his second wife, and resided there with his family from 1907 until his death in July 1930.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>)\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_life">Family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png/170px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png" width="170" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png/255px-Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png 2x" data-file-width="336" data-file-height="501" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arthur_Conan_Doyle_by_George_Wylie_Hutchinson.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arthur Conan Doyle by <a href="/wiki/George_Wylie_Hutchinson" title="George Wylie Hutchinson">George Wylie Hutchinson</a>, 1894</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1885 Doyle married Louisa (sometimes called "Touie") Hawkins (10 April 1857 \xe2\x80\x93 4 July 1906). She was the youngest daughter of J. Hawkins, of <a href="/wiki/Minsterworth" title="Minsterworth">Minsterworth</a>, Gloucestershire, and the sister of one of Doyle\'s patients. Louisa suffered from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Leeman_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leeman-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 1907 he married Jean Elizabeth Leckie (14 March 1874 \xe2\x80\x93 27 June 1940), whom he had first met and fallen in love with in 1897. He had maintained a platonic relationship with Jean while his first wife was still alive, out of loyalty to her.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Jean died in London.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle fathered five children. He had two with his first wife: Mary Louise (28 January 1889 \xe2\x80\x93 12 June 1976) and Arthur Alleyne Kingsley, known as Kingsley (15 November 1892 \xe2\x80\x93 28 October 1918). He had an additional three with his second wife: Denis Percy Stewart (17 March 1909 \xe2\x80\x93 9 March 1955), second husband of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgian</a> Princess <a href="/wiki/Nina_Mdivani" class="mw-redirect" title="Nina Mdivani">Nina Mdivani</a>; <a href="/wiki/Adrian_Conan_Doyle" title="Adrian Conan Doyle">Adrian Malcolm</a> (19 November 1910 \xe2\x80\x93 3 June 1970); and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Conan_Doyle" title="Jean Conan Doyle">Jean Lena Annette</a> (21 December 1912 \xe2\x80\x93 18 November 1997).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>  All of Doyle\'s five children died without issue, leaving him with no grandchildren or direct descendants.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_campaigning">Political campaigning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Political campaigning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Boer War</a> in South Africa at the turn of the 20th century and the condemnation from some quarters over the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>\'s role, Doyle wrote a short work titled <i>The War in South Africa: Its Cause and Conduct</i>, which argued that the UK\'s role in the Boer War was justified, and which was widely translated. Doyle had served as a volunteer doctor in the Langman Field Hospital at <a href="/wiki/Bloemfontein" title="Bloemfontein">Bloemfontein</a> between March and June 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Doyle believed that this publication was responsible for his being <a href="/wiki/Knight#Honorific_orders_of_knighthood" title="Knight">knighted</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">Knight Bachelor</a> by <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">King Edward VII</a> in 1902<sup id="cite_ref-LGKnight_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LGKnight-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and for his appointment as a Deputy-Lieutenant of <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Also in 1900 he wrote a book, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Boer_War" title="The Great Boer War">The Great Boer War</a></i>.\n</p><p>He stood for Parliament twice as a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionist_Party" title="Liberal Unionist Party">Liberal Unionist</a>\xe2\x80\x94in 1900 in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Central_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Edinburgh Central (UK Parliament constituency)">Edinburgh Central</a> and in 1906 in the <a href="/wiki/Hawick_Burghs_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Hawick Burghs (UK Parliament constituency)">Hawick Burghs</a>\xe2\x80\x94but although he received a respectable vote, he was not elected.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Doyle was appointed a Knight of Grace of the <a href="/wiki/Venerable_Order_of_Saint_John" class="mw-redirect" title="Venerable Order of Saint John">Order of the Hospital of Saint John of Jerusalem</a> in 1903.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle was a supporter of the campaign for the reform of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a>, led by the journalist <a href="/wiki/E._D._Morel" title="E. D. Morel">E. D. Morel</a> and diplomat <a href="/wiki/Roger_Casement" title="Roger Casement">Roger Casement</a>. During 1909 he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Crime_of_the_Congo" title="The Crime of the Congo">The Crime of the Congo</a></i>, a long pamphlet in which he denounced the horrors of that colony. He became acquainted with Morel and Casement, and it is possible that, together with <a href="/wiki/Bertram_Fletcher_Robinson" title="Bertram Fletcher Robinson">Bertram Fletcher Robinson</a>, they inspired several characters in the 1912 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(Arthur_Conan_Doyle)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Lost World (Arthur Conan Doyle)">The Lost World</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Doyle broke with Robinson when he became one of the leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifist</a> movement during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a>. When Casement was found guilty of <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a> against <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a>, Doyle tried unsuccessfully to save him from facing the death penalty, arguing that Casement had been driven mad and could not be held responsible for his actions.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Justice_advocate">Justice advocate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Justice advocate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Conandoylestatue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Conandoylestatue.jpg/170px-Conandoylestatue.jpg" width="170" height="368" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Conandoylestatue.jpg/255px-Conandoylestatue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Conandoylestatue.jpg/340px-Conandoylestatue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="879" data-file-height="1905" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Conandoylestatue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Doyle statue in <a href="/wiki/Crowborough" title="Crowborough">Crowborough</a>, East Sussex</div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle was also a fervent advocate of justice and personally investigated two closed cases, which led to two men being exonerated of the crimes of which they were accused. The first case, in 1906, involved a shy half-British, half-Indian lawyer named <a href="/wiki/George_Edalji" title="George Edalji">George Edalji</a> who had allegedly penned threatening letters and mutilated animals in <a href="/wiki/Great_Wyrley" title="Great Wyrley">Great Wyrley</a>. Police were set on Edalji\'s conviction, even though the mutilations continued after their suspect was jailed.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Apart from helping George Edalji, Doyle\'s work helped establish a way to correct other miscarriages of justice, as it was partially as a result of this case that the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Criminal_Appeal" title="Court of Criminal Appeal">Court of Criminal Appeal</a> was established in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The story of Doyle and Edalji was dramatised in an episode of the 1972 BBC television series, <i>The Edwardians</i>. In Nicholas Meyer\'s pastiche <i><a href="/wiki/The_West_End_Horror" title="The West End Horror">The West End Horror</a></i> (1976), Holmes manages to help clear the name of a shy <a href="/wiki/Parsi" title="Parsi">Parsi</a> Indian character wronged by the English justice system. Edalji was of Parsi heritage on his father\'s side. The story was fictionalised in <a href="/wiki/Julian_Barnes" title="Julian Barnes">Julian Barnes</a>\'s 2005 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Arthur_and_George" class="mw-redirect" title="Arthur and George">Arthur and George</a></i>, which was adapted into a three-part drama by ITV in 2015.\n</p><p>The second case, that of <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Slater" title="Oscar Slater">Oscar Slater</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Yekke" title="Yekke">Yekke</a> and gambling-den operator convicted of bludgeoning an 82-year-old woman in <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> in 1908, excited Doyle\'s curiosity because of inconsistencies in the prosecution case and a general sense that Slater was not guilty. He ended up paying most of the costs for Slater\'s successful appeal in 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Spiritualism.2C_Freemasonry"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiritualism,_Freemasonry">Spiritualism, Freemasonry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Spiritualism, Freemasonry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Doyle had a longstanding interest in <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystical</a> subjects.  He was initiated as a <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemason</a> (26 January 1887) at the Phoenix Lodge No. 257 in Southsea. He resigned from the Lodge in 1889, but returned to it in 1902, only to resign again in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Also in Southsea in 1887, influenced by a member of the Portsmouth Literary and Philosophical Society, Major-General <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Wilks_Drayson" title="Alfred Wilks Drayson">Alfred Wilks Drayson</a>, he began a series of psychic investigations. These included attending around 20 <a href="/wiki/Seance" class="mw-redirect" title="Seance">seances</a>, experiments in <a href="/wiki/Telepathy" title="Telepathy">telepathy</a> and sittings with mediums. Writing to <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualist</a> journal <i><a href="/wiki/Light_(spiritualist_journal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Light (spiritualist journal)">Light</a></i>, that year, he declared himself to be a Spiritualist and spoke of one particular psychic event that had convinced him.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though he later wavered, he remained fascinated by the <a href="/wiki/Paranormal" title="Paranormal">paranormal</a>. He was a founding member of the Hampshire Society for Psychical Research in 1889 and joined the London-based <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Psychical_Research" title="Society for Psychical Research">Society for Psychical Research</a> in 1893. He joined Sir Sidney Scott and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Podmore" title="Frank Podmore">Frank Podmore</a> on a poltergeist investigation in Devon in 1894. Nevertheless, during this period, he remained, in essence, a dilettante.<sup id="cite_ref-Wingett32-36_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wingett32-36-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During 1916, at the height of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, a change came over Conan Doyle\'s beliefs prompted by the apparent psychic abilities of his children\'s nanny, Lily Loder Symonds.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This, combined with the deaths he saw around him, made him rationalise that Spiritualism was a "New Revelation"<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> sent by God to bring solace to the bereaved. <i>The New Revelation</i> was the title of his first Spiritualist work, published two years later. In the intervening years, he wrote to <i>Light</i> magazine about his faith and lectured frequently on the truth of Spiritualism.\n</p><p>War-related deaths close to him certainly strengthened his long-held belief in life after death and spirit communication, though it is wrong to claim that the death of his son, Kingsley, turned him to Spiritualism, as is often stated. Doyle came out as a Spiritualist to the public in 1916, a full two years before his son\'s death.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>  It was on 28 October 1918 that Kingsley died from pneumonia contracted during his convalescence after being seriously wounded in the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>. Doyle\'s brother <a href="/wiki/Brigadier-general_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Brigadier-general (United Kingdom)">Brigadier-general</a> Innes Doyle died, also from pneumonia, in February 1919. His two brothers-in-law (one of whom was <a href="/wiki/Ernest_William_Hornung" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernest William Hornung">E. W. Hornung</a>, creator of the literary character <a href="/wiki/A._J._Raffles" title="A. J. Raffles">Raffles</a>) and his two nephews also died shortly after the war.  His second book on Spiritualism, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Vital_Message" title="The Vital Message">The Vital Message</a></i>, appeared in 1919.\n</p><p>Doyle found solace supporting spiritualism and its attempts to find proof of <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">existence beyond the grave</a>.  In particular, according to some,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> he favoured <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism_(beliefs)" title="Spiritualism (beliefs)">Christian Spiritualism</a> and encouraged the <a href="/wiki/Spiritualists%27_National_Union" title="Spiritualists&#39; National Union">Spiritualists\' National Union</a> to accept an eighth precept \xe2\x80\x93 that of following the teachings and example of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus of Nazareth</a>.  He was a member of the renowned supernatural organisation <a href="/wiki/The_Ghost_Club" title="The Ghost Club">The Ghost Club</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg/170px-Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg/255px-Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg/340px-Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg 2x" data-file-width="968" data-file-height="1448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle_and_family.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Doyle with his family in New York City, 1922</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1919 the magician <a href="/wiki/P._T._Selbit" title="P. T. Selbit">P. T. Selbit</a> staged a s\xc3\xa9ance at his own flat in <a href="/wiki/Bloomsbury" title="Bloomsbury">Bloomsbury</a>, with Doyle in attendance.  Some later commentators have stated that he declared the <a href="/wiki/Clairvoyance" title="Clairvoyance">clairvoyance</a> manifestations to be genuine.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>  However, the contemporary report by the <a href="/wiki/Sunday_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunday Express">Sunday Express</a> quotes Doyle as saying: "I should have to see it again before passing a definite opinion on it," and: "I have my doubts about the whole thing".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>  In 1920, Doyle debated the claims of <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a> with the notable sceptic <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCabe" title="Joseph McCabe">Joseph McCabe</a> at Queen\'s Hall in London.  McCabe later published his evidence against the claims of Doyle and Spiritualism in a booklet entitled <i>Is Spiritualism Based on Fraud?</i> which claimed Doyle had been duped into believing Spiritualism by <a href="/wiki/Mediumship" title="Mediumship">mediumship</a> trickery.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle believed that many cases of diagnosed <a href="/wiki/Mental_illness" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a> were the result of <a href="/wiki/Spirit_possession" title="Spirit possession">spirit possession</a>.  He debated the psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Harold_Dearden" title="Harold Dearden">Harold Dearden</a>, who was diametrically opposed to Doyle\'s views.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>  He travelled to Australia and New Zealand on spiritualist missionary work in 1920, and continued his mission all the way up to his death, speaking about his spiritualist conviction in Britain, Europe, and the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Wingett32-36_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wingett32-36-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg/220px-Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg/330px-Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/71/Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="381" data-file-height="305" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cottingley_Fairies_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the five photographs of Frances Griffiths with the <a href="/wiki/Cottingley_Fairies" title="Cottingley Fairies">alleged fairies</a>, taken by Elsie Wright in July 1917</div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle was also inspired by his Spiritualist beliefs to write a novel on the subject, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Land_of_Mist" title="The Land of Mist">The Land of Mist</a></i>, featuring the character Professor Challenger.  He wrote many other non-fictional Spiritualist works; perhaps his most famous being <i>The Coming of the Fairies</i> (1922)<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> which reveals Conan Doyle\'s conviction in the veracity of the five <a href="/wiki/Cottingley_Fairies" title="Cottingley Fairies">Cottingley Fairies</a> photographs. He reproduced them in the book, together with theories about the nature and existence of fairies and spirits. Initially suspected of being falsified, the photos were decades later determined to be faked (along with admissions from the photographers).\n</p><p>Doyle was friends for a time with <a href="/wiki/Harry_Houdini" title="Harry Houdini">Harry Houdini</a>, the American magician who himself became a prominent opponent of the Spiritualist movement in the 1920s following the death of his beloved mother. Although Houdini insisted that Spiritualist mediums employed trickery (and consistently exposed them as frauds), Doyle became convinced that Houdini himself possessed supernatural powers\xe2\x80\x94a view expressed in Doyle\'s <i>The Edge of the Unknown</i>. Houdini was apparently unable to convince Doyle that his feats were simply illusions, leading to a bitter public falling out between the two.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>  A specific incident is recounted in memoirs by Houdini\'s friend <a href="/wiki/Bernard_M._L._Ernst" title="Bernard M. L. Ernst">Bernard M. L. Ernst</a>, in which Houdini performed an impressive trick at his home in the presence of Conan Doyle. Houdini assured Conan Doyle the trick was pure illusion and that he was attempting to prove a point about Doyle not "endorsing phenomena" simply because he had no explanation.  According to Ernst, Conan Doyle refused to believe it was a trick.<sup id="cite_ref-Polidoro_2006_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polidoro_2006-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1922, the psychical researcher <a href="/wiki/Harry_Price" title="Harry Price">Harry Price</a> accused the spirit photographer <a href="/wiki/William_Hope_(paranormal_investigator)" title="William Hope (paranormal investigator)">William Hope</a> of fraud. Doyle defended Hope, but further evidence of trickery was obtained from other researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Doyle threatened to have Price evicted from the <a href="/wiki/National_Laboratory_of_Psychical_Research" title="National Laboratory of Psychical Research">National Laboratory of Psychical Research</a> and claimed if he persisted to write "sewage" about spiritualists, he would meet the same fate as Harry Houdini.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Price wrote "Arthur Conan Doyle and his friends abused me for years for exposing Hope."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Because of the exposure of Hope and other fraudulent spiritualists, Doyle led a mass resignation of eighty-four members of the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Psychical_Research" title="Society for Psychical Research">Society for Psychical Research</a>, as they believed the Society was opposed to spiritualism.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle and spiritualist <a href="/wiki/William_Thomas_Stead" class="mw-redirect" title="William Thomas Stead">William Thomas Stead</a> were duped into believing <a href="/wiki/Julius_and_Agnes_Zancig" title="Julius and Agnes Zancig">Julius and Agnes Zancig</a> had genuine psychic powers, both claiming that the Zancigs used <a href="/wiki/Telepathy" title="Telepathy">telepathy</a>. In 1924 Julius and Agnes Zancig confessed that their <a href="/wiki/Mentalism" title="Mentalism">mind reading</a> act was a trick and published the secret code and all the details of the trick method they had used, under the title <i>Our Secrets!!</i> in a London newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Doyle praised the <a href="/wiki/Psychic" title="Psychic">psychic</a> phenomena and spirit <a href="/wiki/Materialization_(paranormal)" title="Materialization (paranormal)">materializations</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Eusapia_Palladino" title="Eusapia Palladino">Eusapia Palladino</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mina_Crandon" title="Mina Crandon">Mina Crandon</a>, who were both exposed as frauds.<sup id="cite_ref-Houdini_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houdini-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Doyle\'s two-volume book <i>The History of Spiritualism</i> was published in 1926. W. Leslie Curnow, a spiritualist, contributed much research to the book.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In 1926, <a href="/wiki/Robert_John_Tillyard" title="Robert John Tillyard">Robert John Tillyard</a> wrote a predominantly supportive review of Doyle\'s book <i>The History of Spiritualism</i> in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> This caused controversy, and several critics such as <a href="/wiki/Alan_Archibald_Campbell-Swinton" title="Alan Archibald Campbell-Swinton">A. A. Campbell Swinton</a> pointed to the evidence of fraud in mediumship and Doyle\'s non-scientific approach to the subject.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 1927, Doyle spoke in a filmed interview about Sherlock Holmes and spiritualism.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Milner_(historian)" title="Richard Milner (historian)">Richard Milner</a>, an American historian of science, has presented a case that Doyle may have been the perpetrator of the <a href="/wiki/Piltdown_Man" title="Piltdown Man">Piltdown Man</a> hoax of 1912, creating the counterfeit <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">hominid</a> <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> that fooled the scientific world for over 40 years. Milner says that Doyle had a motive\xe2\x80\x94namely, revenge on the scientific establishment for debunking one of his favourite psychics\xe2\x80\x94and that <a href="/wiki/The_Lost_World_(Arthur_Conan_Doyle)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Lost World (Arthur Conan Doyle)"><i>The Lost World</i></a> contains several encrypted clues regarding his involvement in the hoax.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Rosenberg_(writer)" title="Samuel Rosenberg (writer)">Samuel Rosenberg</a>\'s 1974 book <i><a href="/wiki/Naked_is_the_Best_Disguise" class="mw-redirect" title="Naked is the Best Disguise">Naked is the Best Disguise</a></i> purports to explain how, throughout his writings, Doyle left open clues that related to hidden and suppressed aspects of his mentality.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UndershawCirca1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/UndershawCirca1900.jpg/220px-UndershawCirca1900.jpg" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/UndershawCirca1900.jpg/330px-UndershawCirca1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/UndershawCirca1900.jpg/440px-UndershawCirca1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="573" data-file-height="422" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UndershawCirca1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fa\xc3\xa7ade of <a href="/wiki/Undershaw" title="Undershaw">Undershaw</a> with Doyle\'s children, Mary and Kingsley, on the drive</div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle commissioned a newly-built home from Joseph Henry Ball, an architect friend, in 1895, and played an active part of the design process.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> He lived in <i><a href="/wiki/Undershaw" title="Undershaw">Undershaw</a></i> which is near <a href="/wiki/Hindhead" title="Hindhead">Hindhead</a> in <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a> from October 1897 to September 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> It was a hotel and restaurant from 1924 until 2004, when it was bought by a developer and stood empty while conservationists and Doyle fans fought to preserve it.<sup id="cite_ref-Leeman_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leeman-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, the <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court</a> in London ruled the redevelopment permission be quashed because proper procedure had not been followed,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC121112_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC121112-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> but it is now due to become part of the Stepping Stones school for children with disabilities and additional needs.\n</p><p>Doyle was staying at the <a href="/wiki/Glasshayes" title="Glasshayes">Lyndhurst Grand Hotel</a> during March 1912 and made his most ambitious foray into architecture: sketching the original designs for a third storey extension and altering the front facade to the building.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Work began later that year and the building as it is today is a near perfect expression of Doyle\'s plans.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1914, on a family trip to the Jasper National Park in Canada, he designed a golf course and ancillary buildings for a hotel. The plans were realised in full, but neither the golf course nor the buildings have survived.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1926, Doyle laid the foundation stone for a Spiritualist temple in Camden, London. Of the building\'s total \xc2\xa3600 construction costs, he provided \xc2\xa3500.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours_and_awards">Honours and awards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Honours and awards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/File:Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png" class="image"><img alt="Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png/100px-Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png" width="100" height="28" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png/150px-Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png/200px-Knight-Bachelor.ribbon.png 2x" data-file-width="218" data-file-height="60" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Knight_Bachelor" title="Knight Bachelor">Knight Bachelor</a> (1902)</dd>\n<dd><a href="/wiki/File:Order_of_St_John_(UK)_ribbon_-vector.svg" class="image"><img alt="Order of St John (UK) ribbon -vector.svg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Order_of_St_John_%28UK%29_ribbon_-vector.svg/200px-Order_of_St_John_%28UK%29_ribbon_-vector.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="442" data-file-height="126" /></a> Knight of Grace of the <a href="/wiki/Most_Venerable_Order_of_the_Hospital_of_Saint_John_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of Saint John of Jerusalem">Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of Saint John of Jerusalem</a> (1903)</dd>\n<dd><a href="/wiki/File:Queens_South_Africa_Medal_BAR.svg" class="image"><img alt="Queens South Africa Medal BAR.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Queens_South_Africa_Medal_BAR.svg/100px-Queens_South_Africa_Medal_BAR.svg.png" width="100" height="28" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Queens_South_Africa_Medal_BAR.svg/200px-Queens_South_Africa_Medal_BAR.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="218" data-file-height="60" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_South_Africa_Medal" title="Queen&#39;s South Africa Medal">Queen\'s South Africa Medal</a> (1901)</dd>\n<dd><a href="/wiki/File:Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg" class="image"><img alt="Cavaliere OCI BAR.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg/100px-Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg.png" width="100" height="28" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg/150px-Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg/200px-Cavaliere_OCI_BAR.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="218" data-file-height="60" /></a> Knight of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Crown_of_Italy" title="Order of the Crown of Italy">Order of the Crown of Italy</a> (1895)</dd>\n<dd><a href="/wiki/File:Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png" class="image"><img alt="Order of the Medjidie lenta.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png/100px-Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png" width="100" height="27" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png/150px-Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png/200px-Order_of_the_Medjidie_lenta.png 2x" data-file-width="681" data-file-height="187" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Medjidie" title="Order of the Medjidie">Order of the Medjidie</a> \xe2\x80\x93 2nd Class (Ottoman Empire) (1907)</dd></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg/170px-Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg/255px-Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg/340px-Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="876" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Doyle_Arthur_Conan_grave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Doyle\'s grave at <a href="/wiki/Minstead" title="Minstead">Minstead</a></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arthur_Conan_Doyle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068,_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068%2C_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068%2C_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068%2C_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068%2C_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068%2C_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068%2C_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg 2x" data-file-width="625" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-10068,_Schriftsteller_Conan_Doyle_mit_seinem_Sohn.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Doyle in 1930, the year of his death, with his son Adrian</div></div></div>\n<p>Doyle was found clutching his chest in the hall of Windlesham Manor, his house in <a href="/wiki/Crowborough" title="Crowborough">Crowborough</a>, East Sussex, on 7 July 1930. He died of a heart attack at the age of 71. His last words were directed toward his wife: "You are wonderful."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> At the time of his death, there was some controversy concerning his burial place, as he was avowedly not a Christian, considering himself a <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualist</a>. He was first buried on 11 July 1930 in Windlesham rose garden.\n</p><p>He was later reinterred together with his wife in <a href="/wiki/Minstead" title="Minstead">Minstead</a> churchyard in the <a href="/wiki/New_Forest" title="New Forest">New Forest</a>, Hampshire.<sup id="cite_ref-NDB_9-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NDB-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Carved wooden tablets to his memory and to the memory of his wife, originally from the church at Minstead, are on display as part of a Sherlock Holmes exhibition at Portsmouth Museum.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The epitaph on his gravestone in the churchyard reads, in part: "Steel true/Blade straight/Arthur Conan Doyle/Knight/Patriot, Physician, and man of letters".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A statue honours Doyle at Crowborough Cross in Crowborough, where he lived for 23 years.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> There is a statue of Sherlock Holmes in Picardy Place, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, close to the house where Doyle was born.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
